<div id="review_33931872" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33931872" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1568947-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546553013p2/1568947.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/33931872?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/1568947-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781585675609" href="/book/show/949550.The_Warrior_Prophet">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33931872" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10796717161847313418">There weren’t enough superlatives to describe the brilliance of R. Scott Bakker’s first volume in “The Prince of Nothing” trilogy, “The Darkness That Comes Before”. After such an astounding debut, I wondered if the second volume could match the intellectual depth and overall intensity of the first book. Well, “The Warrior-Prophet” more than lives up to the lofty standards set by the previous book, providing one of my favorite fantasy reading experiences ever.<br/><br/>Reading Bakker’s work is like being d</span>
  <span id="freeText10796717161847313418" style="display:none">There weren’t enough superlatives to describe the brilliance of R. Scott Bakker’s first volume in “The Prince of Nothing” trilogy, “The Darkness That Comes Before”. After such an astounding debut, I wondered if the second volume could match the intellectual depth and overall intensity of the first book. Well, “The Warrior-Prophet” more than lives up to the lofty standards set by the previous book, providing one of my favorite fantasy reading experiences ever.<br /><br />Reading Bakker’s work is like being deliciously overwhelmed, caught in a stunning sea of nuances and detail that boggles the mind. It is akin to gazing at a great painting and finding yourself lost in the minute details for hours on end. It is the rare work where I find after finishing a paragraph, I pause to reflect on what I have read, digesting the various ideas and philosophical insights in the book. In that regard, “The Warrior-Prophet” requires active thinking when reading, coaxing the reader to confront and discuss these ideas Bakker presents, an aspect I greatly enjoyed.<br /><br />The Holy War started by Maithanet, the Shriah of the Thousand Temples, continues its march southward to the sacred city of Shimeh. Immense and bloody battles ensue throughout the course of the book between the invading Inrithi factions and the heathen Fanim. Anasûrimbor Kellhus, a mysterious Prince, gradually asserts a greater influence on the Holy War by offering indispensable counsel to the Great Names administrating the war. Furthermore, Kellhus’ remarkably godlike actions and manner begin to transform him in the eyes of the Inrithi from a minor outlying figure into a larger-than-life prophet. But not everyone is pleased with Kellhus’ seemingly divine transformation and the power it brings him.<br /><br />Drusas Achamian is a Mandate sorcerer traveling in company with Kellhus and their two consorts, Esmenet and Serwë. Tortured by apocalyptic dreams that he believes are prophetic, Achamian is convinced that Kellhus is necessary to the success of the Holy War and must be helped. But in assisting Kellhus, Drusas may have to sacrifice everything he has.<br /><br />Bakker crafts a dark and profound tale filled with magnificent battles, glorious world-building, and an immense depth to his characters unseen in fantasy. Kellhus, who is unbelievably godlike in his abilities, is stunning. His penetrating intellect and manipulation of the other characters would come off as ludicrous in the hands of a lesser writer. But with Bakker, Kellhus is a dominating success. <br /><br />The world-building is incredibly deep and innovative with a tremendously imagined historical background. There are some twists and turns, but the plot is mainly straightforward, concentrating on the massive battles of the Holy War. And it is in these war scenes that Bakker does some of his best work, presenting fantastically descriptive and bloody battles that rank up there with some of the best in fantasy. <br /><br />Last Word:<br />“The Warrior-Prophet” is a stunner of a novel, a dark, delicious and deeply engaging masterpiece featuring an incredible amount of depth and painstaking detail. R. Scott Bakker’s “The Prince of Nothing” series is not just stupendous, it is becoming monumental. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10796717161847313418" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11950924607637892382">PROS: Every bit as meticulous, challenging, and absorbing as the first volume. The heart of this story is a Holy War, and I love the honest depiction of just how hard it is to assemble various factions and their sub factions and their sub factions and vaguely shove them in the direction of your enemy with the hopes that too many of your own villages won't get plundered before they read the destination. Magic fully comes into play and is a rich and complicate mixture of mathematics and philosophy</span>
  <span id="freeText11950924607637892382" style="display:none">PROS: Every bit as meticulous, challenging, and absorbing as the first volume. The heart of this story is a Holy War, and I love the honest depiction of just how hard it is to assemble various factions and their sub factions and their sub factions and vaguely shove them in the direction of your enemy with the hopes that too many of your own villages won't get plundered before they read the destination. Magic fully comes into play and is a rich and complicate mixture of mathematics and philosophy that blew my mind. And when we see it fully unleashed, it is awesome in the original sense of the word.<br /><br />CONS: Some will have problems with the female characters, who are often physically taken advantage of. I personally felt is was an honest depiction of the barbaric setting, but I know there are those it won't sit well with.</span>

<div id="review_182628390" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/182628390" />
    <a title="Ian Vance" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5752255-ian-vance"><img alt="Ian Vance" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/182628390?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian Vance" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian Vance" href="/user/show/5752255-ian-vance">Ian Vance</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5752255-ian-vance?shelf=fantasy-sci-fi">fantasy-sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer182628390" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17796827236785290990"><br/><br/><i>“A book was never ‘read.’ Here, as elsewhere, language betrayed the true nature of the activity. To say that a book was read was to make the same mistake as the gambler who crowed about winning as though he’d taken it by force of hand or resolve. To toss the number-sticks was to seize a moment of helplessness, nothing more. But to open a book was by far the more profound gamble. To open a book was not only to seize a moment of helplessness, not only to relinquish a jealous handful of heartbeats </i></span>
  <span id="freeText17796827236785290990" style="display:none"><br /><br /><i>“A book was never ‘read.’ Here, as elsewhere, language betrayed the true nature of the activity. To say that a book was read was to make the same mistake as the gambler who crowed about winning as though he’d taken it by force of hand or resolve. To toss the number-sticks was to seize a moment of helplessness, nothing more. But to open a book was by far the more profound gamble. To open a book was not only to seize a moment of helplessness, not only to relinquish a jealous handful of heartbeats to the unpredictable mark of another man’s quill, it was to allow oneself to be written. For what was a book if not a long consecutive surrender to the movements of another’s soul?”</i> – TWP p. 268<br /><br /><br />I chuckled when I came to the above passage in my dog-eared paperback copy of <i>The Warrior Prophet</i>—highlighted some ten years prior in my first exposure to R. Scott Bakker’s <i>The Second Apocalypse</i>—I chuckled, for this was perhaps the fourth time I’ve re-read this novel/series. How has Bakker, across that decade-long span, written onto my ‘soul’? <br /><br />First, a confession: I am one of the faithful, the damned: we who labor long under the shadow of asoiaf.org’s literature board, arguing as to the merits of Bakker’s work, trading theory after crackpot theory, manufacturing and perpetuating jokes that only the most devoted might draw a chuckle from (or a grimace). This online cult has persisted from Bakker’s first foray into the literary world in 2004, and it is ironically amusing that the most discussed author on George RR Martin’s official message board, aside from GRRM himself, is Bakker--to wit: GRRM, with <i>Game of Thrones</i>, has further burrowed the fantastical into acceptable water-cooler status quo, while Bakker languishes on the sidelines of lower ‘midlist’ as other authors of his generation—the mid-2000’s grimdark explosion—have gone on to carve successful careers, either by prolific consistency (Abercrombie, Erikson) or the lack thereof (Rothfuss, Lynch). Bakker is a slightly different nut to crack, though. He’s rather demanding. Occasionally exhausting. Sometimes gross. Steeped in philosophical training and literary theory, RSB has managed to craft a work that, in my opinion, veers closest in the genre to the perilous ground of ‘important.’ Of course, that easily explains the lack of sales—when you populate a work with characters sublimely wretched (rather than wish-fulfillment); when the goals of the ‘big bad’ eventually seem sympathetic in the light of revelation; when the author climaxes his trilogy with an erudite argument contending that all belief systems are essentially bullshit, illegitimate engines designed to keep societies/groups functioning through violent resource accumulation… we’re a bit of a far cry from the conservative yearnings as typified by a novel like <i>The Return of the King</i>. <br /><br />Reviewing Bakker’s works before I began my fourth re-read, I considered <i>The Warrior Prophet</i> to be the most accomplished and successful of the five novels; and having just completed the first trilogy, that opinion stands. Lacking the world-building onslaught and somewhat alienating structure of the first novel, Bakker is able to plunge immediately into the conflict promised and rapidly expand thematic detail, shaping this onion layer-by-layer. <br /><br />The story, in brief: From a remote mountain sanctuary, a group of monks devote themselves to mastering the various weaknesses of the human condition, the better to dominate circumstances to their inscrutable aim. From this forgotten monastery emerges Kellhus Anasurimbor, initially on a mission to assassinate his father, who threatens the isolation of this cabal. Over the course of the first trilogy, Kellhus infiltrates and eventually yokes the mass force of a Holy War, subverting the religious structures and, it is hinted, being subverted himself by the strength of the mass-mind/world-soul's demand for the savior-hero. Essentially, Kellhus eventually becomes a meme, 're-writing' codes and roles in his mastery of all circumstance (caveat: ..<i>maybe.</i>). Intriguingly, while the monks Kellhus hails from initially seem to be the locus of the trilogy’s internal logic—a cabal of concentrated modernity, rejecting superstition, religion, and belief as simply manifest delusions from ‘the darkness that comes before’—eventually Kellhus learns that it is the Dunyain who are mistaken, that the Gods exist, and damnation is a very real threat. Given the agnostic/atheistic tone constructed across the trilogy, this was an interesting narrative flip, one further developed in the subsequent trilogy The Aspect Emperor.<br /><br />In any case, I merely scratch the surface, and the nice thing about this series is how scratching the surface reveals layers and layers beneath. While completing this novel, excerpts were released for the upcoming sixth volume (of a projected nine or ten), and a few simple lines from one of those pieces made me both re-evaluate sections of the text I’d already read, and glimpse an underlying descriptive theme that carried forth into the 3rd novel.<br /><br />In any case, highest recommendations. While I consider this to be the most rewarding and complete ‘single’ novel of the series, one can consider each series as a novel in and of itself… which, really, is almost a requirement to the long-suffering fans enduring years of contract negotiations and editorial changes just to get ‘the merest fraction’ of the delayed ending.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17796827236785290990" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_182628390'><a id="like_count_review_182628390" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/182628390?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">4 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F18877.The_Warrior_Prophet">Like</a></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_527448104" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/527448104" />
    <a title="Mike Hillcoat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6084443-mike-hillcoat"><img alt="Mike Hillcoat" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/527448104?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike Hillcoat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike Hillcoat" href="/user/show/6084443-mike-hillcoat">Mike Hillcoat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6084443-mike-hillcoat?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer527448104" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6121603683006402639">Many authors over the history of SFF, but especially the last half century, have attempted the saga, a narrative epic stretched across many volumes of books. Arguably, there are many a mythos that have stood the test of a century and more that have anticipated and preceded The Second Apocalypse but few can match the insane talent, training, and ambition of its author, R. Scott Bakker.<br/><br/>Spoilers for The Darkness That Comes Before (TDTCB) below:<br/><br/>The Warrior-Prophet (TWP) opens on a Holy War converged</span>
  <span id="freeText6121603683006402639" style="display:none">Many authors over the history of SFF, but especially the last half century, have attempted the saga, a narrative epic stretched across many volumes of books. Arguably, there are many a mythos that have stood the test of a century and more that have anticipated and preceded The Second Apocalypse but few can match the insane talent, training, and ambition of its author, R. Scott Bakker.<br /><br />Spoilers for The Darkness That Comes Before (TDTCB) below:<br /><br />The Warrior-Prophet (TWP) opens on a Holy War converged, marching south from the Nansur capitol, Momemn. The faithful Intrithi of the Three Seas have answered their Shriah's call to war against the heathen Fanim, the faith of the great nation of Kian, its tribes, and its tributaries. The game of intrigue between the Shriah and the Nansur Emperor has been settled and the Shriah has finally compelled the Emperor to provision the Holy War on pain of religious censure. And so the Holy War marches...<br /><br />TWP continues the story of the characters introduced to the reader in TDTCB, which Bakker has divvied into three separate Marches of the Holy War, each of the characters having found themselves in its violent and tented precincts. Having crossed the steppe entire to answer his father's summons, Kellhus finds himself involved not only in the daylight war of the Inrithi but a war of intrigue with forces ancient, powerful, and hidden. Achamian, the first Mandate Schoolman to find an agent of their enemy in three centuries, becomes divided between his loyalty to his School and to Kellhus, whom he has taken as his student. Conphas, who leads the Emperor's Nansur in battle alongside the Holy War, likewise watches the mysterious Kellhus, convinced that he is an agent of the Fanim come to undo the Holy War from within. Esmenet, believing Achamian spurned her in the canvased alleyways of the Holy War, joins those following in its wake as greater forces around her conspire. Cnaiur, having made a pact with Kellhus to help him murder Kellhus' father and avenge Cnaiur's own, now advises the Holy War's Great Names against the Fanim but obsesses over whether or not Kellhus will keep his promise. And Serwe, captured by the Scylvendi from the Nansur province of Cepalor, finds herself caught in the titanic struggle between Kellhus, who needs Cnaiur's knowledge and silence, and Cnaiur, whose distrust of Kellhus knows no bounds.<br /><br />As the Holy War marches, Bakker's characters are beset by a truly gripping fantasy world, in the vein of Harold Lamb's Iron Men and Saints, where each must decide their faith in a world where belief is settled by sorcery and sword.<br /><br />Bakker's writing is dark, vast, and holy, and his characters deep, brooding souls, as might be expected given the savagery of their world. But yet it allows for hope in the way of implacable evil and characters who strive and fight and endure, despite the overwhelming disparity that oppresses them.<br /><br />Few rare authors will offer you the opportunity to journey through such a fantasy narrative as will R. Scott Bakker. While he remains one of contemporary fantasy's most under read and divisive authors, it's worth engaging and reading on your own terms.<br /><br />Experience it for yourself.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6121603683006402639" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14558438010602821608">5 Stars <br/><br/>This is my reread through book two in The Prince of Nothing series by R. Scott Bakker. The Warrior Prophet was nearly impossible to put down again...Where book one excelled in the dialogue and the philosophy behind the story. This one is an action gem.   <span id="freeText14558438010602821608" style="display:none">5 Stars <br /><br />This is my reread through book two in The Prince of Nothing series by R. Scott Bakker. The Warrior Prophet was nearly impossible to put down again...Where book one excelled in the dialogue and the philosophy behind the story. This one is an action gem. Our two main heroes are front and center through out this intelligent fantasy. Akka follows his beliefs and his man Kellhus. The journey is vast and the action is intense. <br /><br />Kellhus becomes entwined with Cnaiür a tribe chief with revenge on his mind. Many chapters further develop Kellhus into an incredibly scary and nasty warrior. The action is awesome. Many more chapters focus on the magnetism of Kellhus and the devotion of his followers. He truly is the Warrior Prophet.<br /><br />I forgot the major trials and tribulations of my favorite character Achamian. What he goes through and endures in this book is some of the nastiest stuff to read. Wow!<br /><br />I loved this book then, and I love it even more now. It is brilliant. I will go on to reread the concluding book before I start the new series.<br /><br />Fabulous fantasy and writing...</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3468818332953578629">What Bakker began in "The Darkness That Comes Before," he continues to excellent effect in "The Warrior Prophet." I can't imagine a book better tailored to my current tastes and needs, bringing together the strengths of some of my more cherished authors -- Umberto Eco's genius at illuminating history, the dark imagination of a Clark Ashton Smith and Michael Shea's gift for language -- delivered with economic plotting and gifted character craft that allows for very little wasted motion. The book </span>
  <span id="freeText3468818332953578629" style="display:none">What Bakker began in "The Darkness That Comes Before," he continues to excellent effect in "The Warrior Prophet." I can't imagine a book better tailored to my current tastes and needs, bringing together the strengths of some of my more cherished authors -- Umberto Eco's genius at illuminating history, the dark imagination of a Clark Ashton Smith and Michael Shea's gift for language -- delivered with economic plotting and gifted character craft that allows for very little wasted motion. The book just bristles with secrets and wonder, sucking the reader into its world.<br /><br />This is epic, dark fantasy written by a true philosopher. Bakker's world allows you to lose yourself in its rich tapestry, forget who you are for spells -- while other times stirring you to ponder the dimensions of your own self awareness. <br /><br />Great book. </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_339361974" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/339361974" />
    <a title="Ben" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9885298-ben"><img alt="Ben" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1338391313p2/9885298.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/339361974?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ben" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ben" href="/user/show/9885298-ben">Ben</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9885298-ben?shelf=fantasy-noir">fantasy-noir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer339361974" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2118472073052530198">This is an odd one. On the one hand, I want to give this at least three stars for for writing style and an interesting and deep world with a well thought out background. On the other, I want to give this a one star for essentially a rather boring follow up to the much more promising <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/301538.The_Darkness_That_Comes_Before__The_Prince_of_Nothing___1_' title='The Darkness That Comes Before (The Prince of Nothing, #1) by R. Scott Bakker' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Darkness That Comes Before</a>, and an excessive amount of rape and generally unpleasant sex scenes.<br/><br/>Bakker's writing is easy to read and flows well, while at the same time being sophisticated and interesting and very</span>
  <span id="freeText2118472073052530198" style="display:none">This is an odd one. On the one hand, I want to give this at least three stars for for writing style and an interesting and deep world with a well thought out background. On the other, I want to give this a one star for essentially a rather boring follow up to the much more promising <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/301538.The_Darkness_That_Comes_Before__The_Prince_of_Nothing___1_" title="The Darkness That Comes Before (The Prince of Nothing, #1) by R. Scott Bakker" rel="nofollow noopener">The Darkness That Comes Before</a>, and an excessive amount of rape and generally unpleasant sex scenes.<br /><br />Bakker's writing is easy to read and flows well, while at the same time being sophisticated and interesting and very definitely not pulp. He does a good job of painting a scene and he can definitely evoke a readers emotions in regard to his characters; though sadly this seems to be mostly distaste, contempt or general dislike. I have to presume that this is intentional and it is Bakker's design to leave us without even a single character who we can really like in the book. At best we have a few very flawed and grey characters, who often from our outsiders view seem irritatingly stupid. The characters we really do not like go steadily down hill from there, and while I can appreciate that everyone has their problems and a flawed hero can make for a nice change, I do have to actually <i>like</i> at least one of the characters in the book to stay vested in it. Further, if Bakker is going to take the position that the characters have more than one side to them, then I am not sure why he chose to portray the villains in such a ridiculously obvious and unredeamingly evil way; muahahaha, here am I strangling some kittens and then I'll have sex with your dead six-year-old's corpse! While this did not actually happen, it is not so very far off. Perhaps it is only in this way that we can see who the people we are supposed to identify with are.<br /><br />The world is very nicely detailed and we get plenty of culture and background - heavily influenced from Middle Eastern culture, which makes a nice change from the typical medievel setting of many fantasy books. The maps made me thing of Tolkein's straight away, as perhaps this positvely predisposed me towards the world, and I felt that there was more than a token nod towards Tokein in a lot of the naming conventions and the linguistics of the north. Many of the outsider's questions are generally ignored, or only addressed in passing or when the time is really right to talk about them - for example the nature of the magic, the older history of the world and cultures of the lands and peoples which are only names on the map. Personally, I quite like this. When things are artificially explained to you in a novel, I feel patronised and much prefer to go ignorant of some things and enjoy putting the puzzles together myself and to have more nature exposition. Bakker handles this well, I thought.<br /><br />Into the negatives, there is the very basic problem that the plot is really rather boring. I can see <i>why</i> Bakker needs an entire book to develop the character of Kellhus, but to be honest, even when we take into account the monk's super-human abilities to read and manipulate, the whole thing still seems a little rushed and unbelievable in places. The whole story takes place against the backdrop of the the Holy War, as it pushes steadily on towards its goal of liberating the Holy City. Given that this is a major event, you would think that it would be somehow more interesting. Instead it is mostly just background and even when battle scenes occur or dramtic setbacks arise, it is all just so much background and rarely ever has any direct impact or involvement with any of the characters who you are struggling to like. In many ways, at the end of the book, I almost felt that I may as well have skipped this volume, jumped directly to volume three and read the summary in the beginning of the book, and that would have done the job just as well.<br /><br />Finally, I come to the gratuitious and far too frequent unpleasant sex scenes. While there are a couple of depictions of basicly 'normal' sex, almost chapter or every other chapter has some unpleasant rape scene, semi-concensual incest, violent sex, or even borderline beastiality. While not a prude, and not adverse to more adult themes in a book, whether it be for the purposes of mild titilation or for the purposes of provoking an emotional response, I just found this too much and it interfered with my enjoyment of the book. In the end, I was mostly just left wondering if Bakker himself is some kind of closet pervert of the type who should probably be locked up.<br /><br />There <i>are</i> enjoyable elements to this book, and if you were not put off by the first in the series, then you will probably overlook its foibles. In some places I feel it tries a little too hard, mostly in its mimicry of Frank Herbert's Dune and his focus on religion and the Bene Gesserit group which the main character seems to be based on. I probably will get around to reading the last part of this, if only to find out where it is all going, but I cannot say I am really looking forward to it, so it may be a year or two before I pick it up - enough time to wash the unpleasant aftertaste from my mouth.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2118472073052530198" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review339361974">

<div id="review_82982599" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/82982599" />
    <a title="Jurgen_i" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2936065-jurgen-i"><img alt="Jurgen_i" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1269082206p2/2936065.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/82982599?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jurgen_i" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jurgen_i" href="/user/show/2936065-jurgen-i">Jurgen_i</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590201190" href="/book/show/3284049-the-warrior-prophet">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer82982599" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9934871345810402295">I put to the first book 5 points. The Warrior Prophet is also good, sometimes great. But i put only 1 point. Scott Bakker sought to spoil the book with much efforts. A tricky job. I don't know, why.<br/><br/>N.B. This review doesn't contain spoilers, since the main spoiler is the author. The plot is based on events of the first crusade, which ended with crusaders' victory and capture of the holy city. Look at the content: first march, second march, third march. Now you know, holy army won't be defeated un</span>
  <span id="freeText9934871345810402295" style="display:none">I put to the first book 5 points. The Warrior Prophet is also good, sometimes great. But i put only 1 point. Scott Bakker sought to spoil the book with much efforts. A tricky job. I don't know, why.<br /><br />N.B. This review doesn't contain spoilers, since the main spoiler is the author. The plot is based on events of the first crusade, which ended with crusaders' victory and capture of the holy city. Look at the content: first march, second march, third march. Now you know, holy army won't be defeated until the last march. But, somewhere in the first part of the book it becomes obvious that inrithi cannot be defeated and Kelhus should be a victor. At any cost (even the plausibility doesn't matter).<br /><br />The author was trapped by this plot. And the book is such: the holy army goes to Shimeh and defeats again and again superior Fanim forces. At the end Scott Bakker even doesn't bother him to explain how. Last battle looks so: Fanim gathered everyone left in their lands, assembled one more huge army (over 100 000), took 100 battle elephants. They attacked, attacked, attacked, and were defeated. How? Don't know. I'm sure, even Scott Bakker doesn't know and isn't able to explain.<br />To escape the trap and make more interesting, the author creates different obstacles to the Men of the Tusk, but that made things only worse. Many of those obstacles couldn't be overcome in that situation. That's why the author just releases Deus ex machina - and everything is ok.<br /><br />The problem is that the author changed some crucial points, comparing to actual events. Readers, please, do not believe Scott Bakker!!! Muslims weren't united that time. They were departed both religiously and politically (to many small, medium and large state units). Even more, crusaders fought first of all with Seljuks, not Arabs. The absence of unity was the main reason of crusade victory.<br /><br />But the main problem of the book is Anasurimbor Kelhus. I liked this character at the beginning, but then Scott Bakker started nearly worshiping to him, as almost all the characters of the book. Kelhus just become an extremely cheating hero with legions of dei ex machina. A human being just cannot evade things that are moving faster than certain threshold (arrow e.g., except at the end of trajectory or with great luck. It's just anatomy, nothing more. Training doesn't matter. Matrix is matrix. Real world is different, has own laws and rules). But Kelhus evaded dozens or hundreds of arrows, after it killed hundreds of desert warriors. Everything is ok. But not for me and not for logic together with reality.<br /><br />As in lots of pulpy poor fantasies, main villain - the Consult - is very mighty, but very stupid. Isn't it obvious, that it will be defeated? Authors, please, stop doing such stupid things!<br /><br />Ehh, pity, just a pity. This could be a great book, if not cheating and if not Kelhus (how it became in the middle of the book). Don't want to write about positive aspects of the books (there are many) - i'm too upset! Why Scott Bakker did so? Have no idea. I get out of his fans!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9934871345810402295" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_337967411" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/337967411" />
    <a title="agata" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8540972-agata"><img alt="agata" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548665922p2/8540972.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/337967411?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="agata" class="user" itemprop="url" name="agata" href="/user/show/8540972-agata">agata</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9781841494104" href="/book/show/367395.The_Warrior_Prophet">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of fantasy who want to explore psychological and philosophical questions</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to agata by: </span>
      Jasper
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8540972-agata?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8540972-agata?shelf=szafa">szafa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8540972-agata?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer337967411" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4985070066691111203">Everything I said about the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/337967617' rel='nofollow noopener'>first book in the series</a> applies also to the second volume in this triology.<br/><br/>And while it's still all about human psychology Bakker furthers here his exploration with a new theme: Transformation.<br/><br/>In the first volume we met our main protagonists and were introduced to the psychological - and sometimes philosophical - questions they pose. The second volume tells us the story of a transformative event: The Holy War itself.<br/><br/>The Holy War brings agony and pain. It's torture.<br/><br/>An</span>
  <span id="freeText4985070066691111203" style="display:none">Everything I said about the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/337967617" rel="nofollow noopener">first book in the series</a> applies also to the second volume in this triology.<br /><br />And while it's still all about human psychology Bakker furthers here his exploration with a new theme: Transformation.<br /><br />In the first volume we met our main protagonists and were introduced to the psychological - and sometimes philosophical - questions they pose. The second volume tells us the story of a transformative event: The Holy War itself.<br /><br />The Holy War brings agony and pain. It's torture.<br /><br />And while the protagonists struggle through the physical, emotional and spiritual hardships they are transformed. They are beaten, they endure and in the end some are destroyed and some prevail.<br /><br />Achamian - a sceptical sorcerer living in two worlds, Esmenet - a whore who wants to be part of the greater world, Cnaiür - a tribesman on his way to destroy what destroyed his sense of belonging, Proyas - a pious and zealous prince, and Kellhus - a monk who can see what has come before.<br />They all are broken. Every single one of them. And from there on a metamorphosis begins.<br /><br />They all get, what they so desperately went out to get when they decided to take part in this Holy War. But like every good author and observer of the human condition Bakker shows - in some extent very drastically - that getting what you most want will change you in a way you couldn't have ever imagined.<br /><br />And so the second volume of Bakker's triology is about the 'side effects' of transformation. About metamorphosis so profound that it not only changes a person, but also fundamentaly changes the question their life is about.<br /><br /><i>"It is not the answer that enlightens, but the question."</i><br />- Eugène Ionesco</span>
  <a data-text-id="4985070066691111203" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('95g94jfwiM4BhZ6yCgjzDCGxYxJ9Dl6DcCaJrrx1y8y4eDuFwPCDT08IToh/YkqWg5lRsDvqEROyCmgOsz+dDw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('g5+9YBhVwKz73N3f3MNacQcP4FVNuckAqlQTpjoFmarMf7sH71XLLbVRDeWpqePrpSfS9wtdhpBoePIGNU/PaQ==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Common knowledge isn\'t an axiom, it\'s a force of nature. What the masses believe is. But insanity is a weapon, conviction a shield. Delusions give birth to foul new gods.\n\nViolent and dark, the world is filled with the Geisteskranken—men and women whose delusions manifest, twisting reality. High Priest Konig seeks to create order from chaos. He defines the beliefs of his followers, leading their faith to one end: a young boy, Morgen, must Ascend to become a god. A god they can control.\n\nBut there are many who would see this would-be-god in their thrall, including the High Priest’s own Doppels, and a Slaver no one can resist. Three reprobates—The Greatest Swordsman in the World, a murderous Kleptic, and possibly the only sane man left—have their own nefarious plans for the young god.\n\nAs these forces converge on the boy, there’s one more obstacle: time is running out. Decades later, he has witnessed and been part of world-changing events. Yet he has never returned to Machi. Blissfully ignorant of the forces that fuel their prosperity, the people live and work secure in the knowledge that their city is a bastion of progress in a harsh world. For all his power, Heshai is weak, haunted by memories of shame and humiliation. To take Saraykeht, they must first destroy the trade upon which its prosperity is based. Marchat Wilsin, head of Galt\'s trading house in the city, is planning a terrible crime against Heshai and Seedless.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/8587.R_Scott_Bakker">About R. Scott Bakker</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/8587.R_Scott_Bakker"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1222098119p3/8587.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/8587.R_Scott_Bakker">R. Scott Bakker
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,545
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9723907236899089911">Richard Scott Bakker, who writes as <b>R. Scott Bakker</b> and as <b>Scott Bakker</b>, is a novelist whose work is dominated by a large series informally known as the <i>The Second Apocalypse</i> which Bakker began developing whilst as college in the 1980s.<br/><br/>The series was originally planned to be a trilogy, with the first two books entitled <i>The Prince of Nothing</i> and <i>The Aspect-Emperor</i>. However, when Bakker began writin</span>
  <span id="freeText9723907236899089911" style="display:none">Richard Scott Bakker, who writes as <b>R. Scott Bakker</b> and as <b>Scott Bakker</b>, is a novelist whose work is dominated by a large series informally known as the <i>The Second Apocalypse</i> which Bakker began developing whilst as college in the 1980s.<br /><br />The series was originally planned to be a trilogy, with the first two books entitled <i>The Prince of Nothing</i> and <i>The Aspect-Emperor</i>. However, when Bakker began writing the series in the early 2000s, he found it necessary to split each of the three novels into its own sub-series to incorporate all of the characters, themes and ideas he wished to explore. Bakker originally conceived of seven books: a trilogy and two duologies. This later shifted to two trilogies, with the acknowledgement that the third series may yet also expand to a trilogy.<br /><br /><i>The Prince of Nothing</i> trilogy was published between 2003 and 2006. It depicts the story of the Holy War launched by the Inrithi kingdoms against the heathen Fanim of the south to recover the holy city of Shimeh for the faithful. During the war, a man named Ansurimbor Kellhus emerges from obscurity to become an exceptionally powerful and influential figure, and it is discovered that the Consult, an alliance of forces united in their worship of the legendary No-God, a nihilistic force of destruction, are manipulating events to pave the way for the No-God's return to the mortal world.<br /><br />The sequel series, <i>The Aspect-Emperor</i> trilogy, picks up the story twenty years later with Kellhus leading the Inrithi kingdoms in directly seeking out and confronting the Consult. The first novel in this new series is due for publication in 2009.<br /><br />Whilst working on the Prince of Nothing series, Bakker was given a challenge by his wife to write a thriller. To answer this, he produced a science fiction thriller based around a serial killer who can control and influence the human mind. This book, <i>Neuropath</i>, was eventually published in 2008. Inspired, he wrote a second thriller titled <i>The Disciple of the Dog</i> in 2009.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9723907236899089911" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
